[1:0:379,363] Resources  

[2:0:344,406] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and  

[2:1:344,415] restore California's diverse natural and cultural  

[2:2:344,425] resources for current and future generations,  

[3:0:344,435] including operating state parks, managing water  

[3:1:344,445] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging  

[3:2:344,455] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife  

[3:3:344,465] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources.  

[3:4:344,475] Agency programs also protect the public through  

[3:5:344,485] construction and maintenance of levees in the  

[4:0:344,495] Central Valley and suppression of wildfires. These  

[4:1:344,505] programs not only contribute to the state's unique  

[4:2:344,514] quality of life, but also are critical to sustaining a  

[4:3:344,524] vibrant economy.

[1:0:242,39] 3100   Exposition Park  

[2:0:66,60] The Office of Exposition Park Management, the Science Center, and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are  

[2:1:66,70] located in Exposition Park, a 152-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these  

[2:2:66,79] three departments are collectively known as Exposition Park.  

[3:0:66,97] The Office of Exposition Park Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park  

[3:1:66,106] usage policy and day-to-day management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of  

[3:2:66,116] the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science learning. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect, preserve,  

[3:3:66,126] and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of African Americans with an emphasis on California and the  

[3:4:66,136] western United States.  

[4:0:66,153] Because   the Science Center's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the   Science Center   has a capital outlay  

[4:1:66,163] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Science Center's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,331] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:0:66,350] 2300 - CALIFORNIA SCIENCE CENTER  

[9:0:66,370] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the California Science Center represents one of California's premier educational and  

[9:1:66,380] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops  

[9:2:66,389] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs.  

[10:0:66,409] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological  

[10:1:66,419] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering,  

[10:2:66,428] communications, and transportation, on land and in space.   Construction for the Science Center’s Air and Space Center, the  

[10:3:66,438] permanent home for the Space Shuttle Endeavour, began in March 2022 and is expected to be completed in spring 2025.  

[11:0:66,458] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The  

[11:1:66,468] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and  

[11:2:66,477] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a  

[11:3:66,487] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The  

[11:4:66,497] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards.  

[12:0:66,516] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those  

[12:1:66,526] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding  

[12:2:66,536] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation.  

[13:0:66,553] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT  

[14:0:66,573] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management  

[14:1:66,583] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events,  

[14:2:66,592] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is  

[14:3:66,602] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of  

[14:4:66,612] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park  

[14:5:66,622] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in  

[14:6:66,631] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood.  

[15:0:66,649] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM  

[16:0:66,666] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the  

[16:1:66,676] Governor. CAAM’s mission is to research, collect, preserve, and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of  

[16:2:66,686] African Americans with an emphasis on California and the western United States. Founded in 1977, as the first African  

[16:3:66,695] American museum of art, history, and culture fully supported by a state, CAAM was the direct result of a sustained, multiyear  

[16:4:66,705] campaign of activism undertaken by visionary founders and community members. CAAM occupies a 44,000-square-foot facility  

[16:5:66,715] in Exposition Park, which includes 15,000 square feet of gallery space and an additional 13,000 square feet of space for public  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] programming. The museum’s permanent collection houses 4,000 objects that span paintings, photographs, film, sculpture,  

[3:1:66,78] historical documents, and artifacts. CAAM has a strong mandate to support the work of under-represented artists of color and  

[3:2:66,88] to think expansively about the way that people of the African diaspora have contributed to American culture and the world at  

[3:3:66,98] large. That is achieved through a rotating exhibition schedule and public programming for visitors of all ages. More than forty  

[3:4:66,108] years after its founding, the originating principle for CAAM remains vital to a healthy and just America--to create and support an  

[3:5:66,117] inclusive, accessible, and dynamic space where all people can see black lives, culture, and experiences valued and reflected.

[6:0:66,303] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,322] The Office of Exposition Park Management oversees Exposition Park, a 152-acre state property, in Los Angeles which hosts  

[7:1:66,331] the   Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum,   Bank of Montreal Stadium, the   California Science Center, the   Natural History Museum of  

[7:2:66,341] Los Angeles County, the   California African American Museum, the Los Angeles City Rose Garden and Expo Center, and the  

[7:3:66,351] future Lucas Museum of Narrative Art. State property also includes green, open spaces including the South Lawn, Expo Lawn,  

[7:4:66,361] Christmas Tree Lane, Jesse Brewer Jr. Park, Soboroff Playfield, and on-site infrastructure including parking structures and

[5:1:66,157] 2320 - TAHOE REGIONAL PLANNING AGENCY  

[6:0:66,177] A congressionally approved compact between California and Nevada established the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency. The  

[6:1:66,186] Agency coordinates planning and regulations that preserve and enhance the environment and resources of the Lake Tahoe  

[6:2:66,196] Basin. Funding for the Agency is shared between Nevada (one-third) and California (two-thirds).

[7:1:66,243] The funds are allocated by the State Controller for restoration and preservation projects in Yosemite National Park.  

[7:2:66,260] 2330 - SEA GRANT PROGRAM

[8:1:66,289] provides funding to the University of California and University of Southern California to match federal funds for selected  

[8:2:66,299] projects under the federal Sea Grant Program.

[6:0:201,187] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy  

[7:0:66,208] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the natural and recreational  

[7:1:66,218] resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin (Basin). The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and implementing programs  

[7:2:66,228] that acquire environmentally sensitive land; restoring the thousands of parcels it owns to improve stream environments, wildlife  

[7:3:66,238] habitats, forest health, and water quality; and providing grants to fund soil erosion prevention, watershed restoration, improve  

[7:4:66,247] forest health, and reduce wildfire risk on other public lands. The Conservancy balances these activities with programs to  

[7:5:66,257] improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally sound manner by acquiring lakefront property,  

[7:6:66,267] constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities.  

[8:0:66,284] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP), which is intended to  

[8:1:66,294] restore and protect the Basin's natural resources. The EIP leverages funding from the states of California and Nevada, the  

[8:2:66,304] Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and various private entities for projects needed  

[8:3:66,313] to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[8:4:66,323] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects.  

[9:0:66,340] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program  

[9:1:66,350] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[9:0:66,418] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:1:66,437] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY  

[10:0:66,454] The Conservancy manages nearly 4,700 state-owned properties totaling more than 6,500 acres, including thousands of  

[10:1:66,464] quarter-acre lots within the Basin’s urban areas. These parcels provide open space, water quality and recreational benefits,  

[10:2:66,473] and have significantly reduced the potential level of development in the Basin. The Conservancy manages its lands for multiple  

[10:3:66,483] benefits and inspects each parcel once every two years to monitor forest health and to identify maintenance needs and  

[10:4:66,493] encroachments. Maintaining healthy forests in urban areas to help protect life and property constitutes another fundamental  

[10:5:66,503] role of the Conservancy’s land management program. The Conservancy also manages 11 properties with recreational  

[10:6:66,513] amenities, including 6 beaches that provide public access to Lake Tahoe.  

[11:0:66,530] The Conservancy and its partners are increasingly shifting to large landscape, multi-jurisdictional, multiple-benefit projects to  

[11:1:66,540] more rapidly and comprehensively achieve restoration goals in the Basin. The Conservancy plans to continue working with a  

[11:2:66,549] wide variety of partners to restore the health and resilience of the Basin’s forests and watersheds, with the aim of also  

[11:3:66,559] increasing the pace and scale of restoration. This includes improving forest diversity, protecting wildlife, applying beneficial fire,  

[11:4:66,569] clearing meadows of encroaching trees, reducing streambank erosion, reconnecting floodplains, and treating storm water. This  

[11:5:66,579] work will enhance the ability of these lands to respond to wildfire, drought, insects, and climate change.  

[12:0:66,596] The Conservancy also works closely with local governments to meet its mission by providing grants for acquisitions and site  

[12:1:66,606] improvements such as building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment basins, building bike trails, and creating  

[12:2:66,615] public gathering spaces and improving public access to the Lake.  

[12:3:66,633] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS  

[13:0:66,652] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing  

[13:1:66,662] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide  

[13:2:66,672] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and watersheds  

[13:3:66,682] such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality.  

[13:4:66,699] In addition, the program is helping to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and achieve environmental benefits by promoting  

[13:5:66,709] urban in-fill development in the Basin. These efforts include selling, leasing, or exchanging non-environmentally sensitive  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] Conservancy lands and associated development rights to support revitalization efforts consistent with local area plans and town  

[3:1:66,78] center objectives.   Property exchanges and the proceeds from the sale or leasing of property are then used to (1) acquire other  

[3:2:66,88] environmentally sensitive lands or (2) fund conservation and other projects that support the Conservancy in fulfilling its mission.

[3:0:66,69] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[4:0:66,87] The California Tahoe Conservancy manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife habitat,  

[4:1:66,97] watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary implementer  

[4:2:66,107] of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake Tahoe Basin - a  

[4:3:66,117] 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local  

[4:4:66,127] governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[4:5:66,136] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and new projects.

[11:0:188,569] 3210   Environmental Protection Program  

[12:0:66,590] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates  

[12:1:66,600] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds  

[12:2:66,610] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes:  

[12:3:65,627] • The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of  

[12:4:76,637] environmental pollutants.  

[13:0:65,653] • The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves.  

[13:1:65,668] • Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs.  

[13:2:65,684] • Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals.  

[13:3:65,700] • Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the  

[13:4:76,710] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:153,32] 3210   Environmental Protection Program - Continued 

[3:0:65,68] • The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and for  

[3:1:76,78] local and regional parks.  

[4:0:65,94] • Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe  

[4:1:76,104] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones.  

[5:0:66,131] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate Fund  

[5:1:66,141] are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments.

[13:0:200,526] 3340   California Conservation Corps  

[13:1:66,547] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires,  

[13:2:66,557] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation measures at  

[13:3:66,566] public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic skills to become  

[13:4:66,576] strong workers and citizens.  

[14:0:66,596] In addition to the CCC, there are also 14 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state that  

[14:1:66,606] are annually certified by the CCC, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, education, and training activities.  

[15:0:66,625] Because the   CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the CCC has a capital outlay program to support this  

[15:1:66,635] need. For the specifics on the CCC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,279] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,298] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,315] This Program focuses on five areas:  

[6:0:66,332] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail  

[6:1:66,342] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native  

[6:2:66,352] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant  

[6:3:66,361] and other landscaping.  

[7:0:66,379] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods,  

[7:1:66,388] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance  

[7:2:66,398] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security  

[7:3:66,408] assistance.  

[8:0:66,425] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC through  

[8:1:66,435] local adult and charter schools and community colleges. 

[9:0:66,452] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which includes  

[9:1:66,462] teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles and career  

[9:2:66,472] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and  

[9:3:66,482] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California. 

[9:4:66,499] Corpsmember Career Pathways: The CCC dedicates resources to help Corpsmembers transition out of the program to  

[9:5:66,509] meaningful career pathways, including but not limited to: higher education, trade schools, apprenticeships, careers with local/ 

[9:6:66,518] state/federal agencies, etc., to apply their skillsets and build financial independence.

[7:0:66,344] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[8:0:66,363] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 27 facilities statewide, including 10 residential and 16 non-residential  

[8:1:66,372] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities enable the CCC to achieve its mission of providing employment, training,  

[8:2:66,382] and educational opportunities to young adults. The facilities also allow the CCC to assist federal, state and local agencies, as  

[8:3:66,392] well as nonprofit entities, with the conservation of California's natural resources.  

[9:0:66,409] The CCC residential facilities house 80 to 100 corpsmembers each and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people  

[9:1:66,419] together from across the state, and increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential  

[9:2:66,429] centers range in size from 30 to 60 corpsmembers and are located in both urban and rural communities throughout California.

[9:0:66,463] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:1:66,482] 2370 - REGULATION OF ENERGY INFRASTRUCTURE SAFETY  

[10:0:66,500] Established pursuant to Chapter 81, Statutes of 2019 (AB 111), the Office is the successor to the Wildfire Safety Division of the  

[10:1:66,509] California Public Utilities Commission as of July 1, 2021 and is authorized to:  

[11:0:65,527] • Oversee and enforce electrical corporations’ compliance with wildfire safety pursuant to Chapter 6 (commencing with Section  

[11:1:76,536] 8385) of Division 4.1.  

[12:0:65,552] • In consultation with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board, develop and recommend to the commission performance  

[12:1:76,562] metrics to achieve maximum feasible risk reduction to be used to develop the wildfire mitigation plan and evaluate an  

[12:2:76,572] electrical corporation’s compliance with that plan.  

[13:0:65,587] • Develop a field audit program for wildfire mitigation plan compliance by each electrical corporation.  

[13:1:65,603] • Consult with the Office of Emergency Services in the office’s management and response to utility public safety power shutoff  

[13:2:76,613] events and utility actions for compliance with public safety power shutoff program rules and regulations.  

[13:3:65,629] • Support efforts to assess and analyze fire weather data and other atmospheric conditions that could lead to catastrophic  

[13:4:76,639] wildfires and to reduce the likelihood and severity of wildfire incidents that could endanger the safety of persons, properties,  

[13:5:76,648] and the environment within the state.  

[14:0:65,664] • Retain appropriate staff that includes experts in wildfire, weather, climate change, emergency response, and other relevant  

[14:1:76,674] subject matters.  

[15:0:65,690] • Review, as necessary, in coordination with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board and necessary commission staff,  

[15:1:76,699] safety requirements for electrical transmission and distribution infrastructure and infrastructure and equipment attached to  

[15:2:76,709] that electrical infrastructure, and provide recommendations to the commission to address the dynamic risk of climate change  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:70,32] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,87] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,115] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program  

[5:1:66,124] certifies power plant sites, develops and implements landscape-scale plans to identify suitable areas for renewable energy  

[5:2:66,134] resources and infrastructure development, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel  

[5:3:66,144] infrastructure for compliance and supply and distribution adequacy, consistent with state energy policies. The program  

[5:4:66,154] develops state energy policy based on the economic, security, safety, and environmental land-use and climate implications of  

[5:5:66,163] energy supply and demand. The Energy Assessments program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and  

[5:6:66,173] demand, maintains current information on statewide electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas,  

[5:7:66,183] transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial,  

[5:8:66,193] security, safety, and environmental implications of supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets  

[5:9:66,202] for competitive prices and potential market abuses.  

[6:0:66,220] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[7:0:66,237] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation programs are responsible for the following:  

[7:1:66,247] reviewing thermal power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant owners, operators, and  

[7:2:66,257] developers comply with their specific conditions of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing,  

[7:3:66,266] operating, and closing power plants, and investigating potential violations; reviewing project change petitions to amend filed  

[7:4:66,276] applications; reviewing applications for transmission line corridor designation; developing environmental and land use  

[7:5:66,286] information and evaluating constraints and opportunities for renewable energy infrastructure development; and analyzing  

[7:6:66,296] safety, reliability, efficiency, resilience, and environmental performance issues related to power plants and the state electricity  

[7:7:66,305] transmission grid.  

[8:0:66,323] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis  

[9:0:66,340] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and  

[9:1:66,350] infrastructure needs; estimating costs and emissions of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity  

[9:2:66,360] supplies; and analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the  

[9:3:66,369] distribution system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides  

[9:4:66,379] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program  

[9:5:66,389] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the  

[9:6:66,399] adequacy of natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability,  

[9:7:66,408] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor short-

[9:8:66,418] term natural gas market trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply and  

[9:9:66,428] production, fuel infrastructure issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies.  

[10:0:66,445] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,472] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity  

[11:1:66,482] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently  

[11:2:66,492] using energy in all sectors.  

[12:0:66,509] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program  

[13:0:66,526] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak  

[13:1:66,536] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency and flexible demand standards for appliances,  

[13:2:66,546] equipment, and new and existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides  

[13:3:66,556] technical assistance on building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further,  

[13:4:66,565] the program maintains a database of efficient appliances and equipment and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency  

[13:5:66,575] standards.  

[14:0:66,593] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program  

[15:0:66,610] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce  

[15:1:66,620] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities.  

[16:0:66,637] 2385028 - Demand Analysis  

[17:0:66,654] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to  

[17:1:66,664] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of  

[17:2:66,674] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program conducts detailed electricity and natural gas end-

[17:3:66,683] use surveys which serve as a primary data source for energy demand forecasts, conservation potential studies, and market  

[17:4:66,693] and policy impact assessments. The program also performs transportation energy demand forecasting, conducts consumer  

[17:5:66,703] vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California electricity use.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — RES   33 

[2:0:70,32] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,96] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support  

[4:1:66,105] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public  

[4:2:66,115] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration,  

[4:3:66,125] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and  

[4:4:66,135] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are: cost effective, secure,  

[4:5:66,144] and reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides  

[4:6:66,154] technical assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology  

[4:7:66,164] analysis and evaluation, and knowledge sharing.  

[4:8:66,181] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels  

[5:0:66,198] The Clean Transportation Program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies, infrastructure,  

[5:1:66,208] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change and public health  

[5:2:66,218] policies. The program goal is the development, deployment and integration of zero and near-zero emission fuels and advanced  

[5:3:66,228] vehicle technologies and infrastructure. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state energy policy and  

[5:4:66,238] legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote economic  

[5:5:66,247] development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development, training, and manufacturing that supports the  

[5:6:66,257] program goals.  

[6:0:66,274] 2390019 - Research and Development  

[7:0:66,292] The Research and Development program spurs innovation in energy efficiency, energy generation, storage, infrastructure  

[7:1:66,301] resiliency, renewable integration, and transportation to meet the state’s clean energy and climate goals. The programs bring  

[7:2:66,311] science and technology breakthroughs from the lab to the market to benefit all Californians, with a focus on ensuring the  

[7:3:66,321] benefits also accrue to under-resourced communities. The programs catalyze a world-class innovation ecosystem that provides  

[7:4:66,331] the resources, expertise, and information to foster and support successful clean energy entrepreneurship across the state. The  

[7:5:66,341] programs equip communities, businesses, and public agencies with breakthrough technology solutions and tools to build a  

[7:6:66,350] safe, resilient, affordable and clean energy system. The programs are administered through strategic performance-based  

[7:7:66,360] competitive solicitations.  

[7:8:66,377] 2390028 - Renewable Energy  

[8:0:66,395] The Renewable Energy program fosters growth of the renewable energy market by providing financial incentives to support the  

[8:1:66,404] development and use of eligible renewable energy products; requiring utilities to disclose information to consumers on their  

[8:2:66,414] power fuel mix; implementing the Renewables Portfolio Standard; and establishing solar equipment rating standards and  

[8:3:66,424] maintaining solar equipment lists meeting the performance and safety standards.  

[8:4:66,441] 2395 - LOAN REPAYMENT PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,468] The Loan Repayment program consists of Conservation and Development program loan repayments deposited into the  

[9:1:66,478] following accounts: State Energy Conservation Assistance Account; Energy Technologies Research, Development and  

[9:2:66,488] Demonstration Account; and Local Government Geothermal Resources Revolving Subaccount.  

[10:0:66,527] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM  

[11:0:408,549] 2022-23* 2023-24* 2024-25*

[7:0:66,672] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,691] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION

# === PAGE 58 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — RES   56 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:171,32] 3480   Department of Conservation - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides,  

[3:1:66,78] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and  

[3:2:66,88] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets; and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal,  

[3:3:66,98] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about  

[3:4:66,108] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development.  

[3:5:66,125] 2425 - GEOLOGIC ENERGY MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[4:0:66,142] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to prevent, as   much  

[4:1:66,152] as possible, damage to life, health, property, and natural resources. The program seeks to protect public health and safety and  

[4:2:66,162] environmental quality, including reduction and mitigation of greenhouse gas emissions associated with the development of  

[4:3:66,171] hydrocarbon and geothermal resources in a manner that meets the energy needs of the state. The state is fully reimbursed for  

[4:4:66,181] program expenditures by annual assessments and fees on the respective industries. 

[4:5:66,198] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION  

[5:0:66,216] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. The Williamson Act program  

[5:1:66,226] provides advice on and reviews documents related to changes to Williamson Act contracts between landowners and local  

[5:2:66,235] governments. The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts  

[5:3:66,245] for planning purposes and for the acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development  

[5:4:66,255] rights, and therefore development pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program develops  

[5:5:66,265] maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to assist in local land use  

[5:6:66,274] decisions.  

[6:0:66,292] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION  

[7:0:66,309] This program regulates surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the Surface  

[7:1:66,319] Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to reclaim  

[7:2:66,329] mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned mines and  

[7:3:66,338] remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety.  

[7:4:66,356] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD  

[8:0:66,373] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of  

[8:1:66,383] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The department's California Geological Survey and the Division of Mine  

[8:2:66,392] Reclamation provide the Board with relevant engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for certain reports, plans,  

[8:3:66,402] and maps. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notice of violation orders to  

[8:4:66,412] comply, or administrative penalties and in cases where the Division of Mine Reclamation contests the adequacy of a local  

[8:5:66,422] government’s approval of a mine operation’s financial assurance cost estimate.  

[9:0:66,458] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM  †

[4:0:164,143] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection  

[5:0:66,163] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) provides all hazard—fire, medical, rescue, and  

[6:0:66,173] disaster—emergency response to the public and provides leadership in the protection of life, property, and natural resources in  

[6:1:66,183] California.  

[7:0:66,203] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety  

[7:1:66,212] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information and education; resource protection; and emergency  

[7:2:66,222] response.  

[8:0:66,242] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing  

[8:1:66,251] diverse yet complementary efforts, including: training California's fire service professionals, public education and prevention  

[8:2:66,261] awareness, responsible stewardship of our natural resources, and natural resource and emergency management.  

[9:0:66,281] CAL FIRE cultivates mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels of government and the private sector to  

[9:1:66,291] enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate emergencies in the urban, rural, and wildland  

[9:2:66,300] areas of California.  

[10:0:66,320] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investments, CAL FIRE has a capital outlay program to support  

[10:1:66,330] this need. For the specifics on the CAL FIRE's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,138] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,157] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL  

[7:0:66,174] The Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) protects life and property through the development and application of fire  

[7:1:66,184] prevention, engineering, education, enforcement, and regulations.  

[8:0:66,201] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal:  

[9:0:66,219] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is comprised of the following six divisions:  

[10:0:65,236] • Code Development and Analysis: The Code Development and Analysis division is responsible for the development and  

[10:1:76,246] adoption of codes relating to fire and life safety used statewide by architects, engineers, design professionals, and the local  

[10:2:76,256] fire and building authorities. This division fosters, promotes, and develops ways and means of protecting life and property  

[10:3:76,265] against fire in many ways, including the adoption and implementation of regulations for statewide application. This division  

[10:4:76,275] prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and building standards for review and adoption into the California Code of  

[10:5:76,285] Regulations, titles 19 and 24. The division assists local fire departments, fire districts, and building departments with the  

[10:6:76,295] application of state laws and regulations. This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life safety  

[10:7:76,304] for relevancy, necessity, conflict, duplication, and overlap.  

[11:0:65,320] • Fire and Life Safety: The Fire and Life Safety division is responsible for applying laws and regulations related to fire  

[11:1:76,330] prevention and life safety in state-owned and state-occupied facilities. This is achieved through code compliance inspections  

[11:2:76,340] of new and existing buildings and plan review and construction inspections. The OSFM is responsible for fire and life safety  

[11:3:76,350] in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, conservation camps, courts, state  

[11:4:76,359] mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California State University and University of California campuses, and  

[11:5:76,369] California Agricultural Districts.  

[12:0:65,385] • Fire Engineering and Investigations: The Fire Engineering and Investigations division’s primary functions include licensing,  

[12:1:76,395] product approval, fire/explosion investigations, and illegal fireworks enforcement and disposal. The division is responsible for  

[12:2:76,404] 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire Extinguishers; Flame Retardant Chemicals  

[12:3:76,414] and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire Safe Cigarettes; Licensing Enforcement and  

[12:4:76,424] Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Motion Picture and Entertainment; Arson and Bomb Investigation; and Fireworks Disposal.  

[12:5:76,434] This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the responsibility to dispose of seized illegal fireworks and  

[12:6:76,443] conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement.  

[13:0:65,459] • Pipeline Safety: The Pipeline Safety division ensures the safe construction, operation, and maintenance of approximately  

[13:1:76,469] 5,400 miles of intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport crude oil, refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel,  

[13:2:76,479] etc.), and Highly Volatile Liquids through populated urban areas, ecologically sensitive areas, and other high consequence  

[13:3:76,489] areas. Pipelines that fall under the Pipeline Safety division authority are pipelines that transport hazardous liquids between  

[13:4:76,498] facilities, such as offshore platforms, production fields, refineries, storage terminals, and marine terminals. This division is  

[13:5:76,508] additionally charged with implementing investigations for failures, explosions, and fires on intrastate pipelines, and assisting  

[13:6:76,518] on investigations for interstate pipelines. The Certified Unified Program Agencies program is responsible for ensuring the  

[13:7:76,528] implementation of the California Fire Code Hazardous Materials Management Plan/Hazardous Materials Inventory  

[13:8:76,537] Statement (HMMP/HMIS) and the Aboveground Petroleum Storage Act program elements. The HMMP/HMIS program ties in  

[13:9:76,547] closely with the Hazardous Release Response Plan and Inventory or Hazardous Materials Business Plan program. The  

[13:10:76,557] Firefighter Equipment and Foam program is tasked to monitor the use of Perfluoroalky and Polyfluoroalkyl substances  

[13:11:76,567] (PFAS) throughout the state at designated facilities, and issue waivers to refineries and terminals for fixed suppression  

[13:12:76,577] systems meeting identified criteria.  

[14:0:65,592] • State Fire Training: The State Fire Training division administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System  

[14:1:76,602] and the Fire Service Training and Education Program for federal, state, and local firefighters. The division oversees a  

[14:2:76,612] California Fire Academy System for over 65 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments,  

[14:3:76,622] community colleges, and the OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for fire service  

[14:4:76,631] personnel and receives program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services.  

[15:0:65,647] • Wildfire Planning and Engineering: The Wildfire Planning and Engineering division will be transitioning into the new  

[15:1:76,657] Community Wildfire Preparedness and Mitigation Division established by the passage of Chapter 225, Statutes of 2021 (AB  

[15:2:76,667] 9). The division is responsible for various community-level fire prevention and preparedness programs including defensible  

[15:3:76,677] space, home hardening, fire planning, land use planning, utility fire mitigation, fire prevention grants, fire hazard mapping,  

[15:4:76,686] incident data and statistics, and damage inspection.  

[16:0:66,704] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board:  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 75 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — RES   73 

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] Also called the “Dig Safe Board,” created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), the Board works on  

[3:1:66,78] issues related to the state's "Call Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators and buried infrastructure owners to coordinate  

[3:2:66,88] to prevent infrastructure damage and unsafe outcomes. The Dig Safe Board is charged with coordinating the state’s safe  

[3:3:66,98] excavation outreach and education efforts, developing safe excavation standards, investigating accidents and working with  

[3:4:66,108] partner state agencies to enforce the “Call Before You Dig” law. The Dig Safe Board was transferred to the Office of Energy  

[3:5:66,117] Infrastructure Safety on January 1, 2022 to comply with the requirements of Chapter 307, Statutes of 2020 (SB 865).  

[4:0:66,135] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION  

[5:0:66,152] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property, and natural resources within social, political, and economic  

[5:1:66,162] constraints. The objective is to attack fires quickly and aggressively in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct  

[5:2:66,171] protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract, or agreement until the fire is under control. The level of initial attack and  

[5:3:66,181] follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires within the first  

[5:4:66,191] burning period.  

[6:0:66,208] 2465010 - Fire Prevention:  

[7:0:66,226] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression  

[7:1:66,235] cost recovery, and fire prevention education. This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials, and procedures to  

[7:2:66,245] mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires. More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to be  

[7:3:66,255] done before a wildland fire starts to reduce the costs of firefighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire fighters, and  

[7:4:66,265] damage to the environment.  

[8:0:66,282] 2465019 - Fire Control:  

[9:0:66,299] The objective of this program is to detect, respond to, and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility  

[9:1:66,309] Area. The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy. CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to  

[9:2:66,319] ten acres or less. This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack, and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews,  

[9:3:66,329] bulldozers, helicopters, and fixed wing aircraft.  

[10:0:66,346] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection:  

[11:0:66,363] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state, and federal agencies throughout California  

[11:1:66,373] through the administration of over 100 cooperative fire protection service agreements. Additionally, there are six long standing  

[11:2:66,383] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department.  

[12:0:66,400] 2465037 - Conservation Camps:  

[13:0:66,417] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Parole Operations and  

[13:1:66,427] Division of Juvenile Justice, operates 30 conservation camps throughout the state with capacity to house 152 fire crews. CAL  

[13:2:66,437] FIRE operates seven permanent Fire Centers, six seasonal Fire Centers, and one Parole Training Center with the California  

[13:3:66,446] Conservation Corps throughout the state that house 28 fire crews. CAL FIRE also operates in conjunction with the California  

[13:4:66,456] National Guard to staff seven Fire Centers with a total of 10 fire crews.   Additionally, CAL FIRE is currently authorized to  

[13:5:66,466] operate 16 CAL FIRE Firefighter Crews located at seven different Fire Centers. These crews are available to respond to all  

[13:6:66,476] types of emergencies including wildfires, floods, search and rescue, and earthquakes. When not responding to emergencies,  

[13:7:66,486] the crews engage in hazardous fuels reduction, conservation related work projects, and prescribed fire project work to protect  

[13:8:66,495] the community and resources of California.  

[14:0:66,513] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression:  

[15:0:66,530] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope  

[15:1:66,540] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period.   Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was  

[15:2:66,549] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management  

[15:3:66,559] Agency or through cost shares provided by other government entities. In addition, CAL FIRE may recover portions of this cost  

[15:4:66,569] through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes.  

[16:0:66,586] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT  

[17:0:66,604] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands, and urban forests provide multiple human, climate, and  

[17:1:66,613] environmental benefits. The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to  

[17:2:66,623] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development. Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting,  

[17:3:66,633] coordination of climate and forest restoration related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial  

[17:4:66,643] landowners, operation of state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects, and  

[17:5:66,652] administration of federal forestry assistance programs.  

[18:0:66,670] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement:  

[19:0:66,687] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests. Activities include: (1) forest restoration through fuels  

[19:1:66,697] reduction and fire reintroduction; (2) the detection, evaluation and control of forest pests; (3) growing and selling tree seedlings  

[19:2:66,707] for reforestation, carbon sequestration through reforestation, biomass utilization, and wood products development; (4) avoided

[3:2:66,88] conservation practices; (8) research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state forests; (9) implementation of the  

[3:3:66,98] California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; (10) technical assistance and grants to foster the environmental, social, and  

[3:4:66,108] economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and (11) purchase and monitoring  

[3:5:66,117] of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008. In the area of vegetation management,  

[3:6:66,127] assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by reducing damage from wildland fires,  

[3:7:66,137] increase wildlife habitat, increase productivity of forest and rangelands, improve water yields and air quality, reduce carbon  

[3:8:66,147] emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable ecosystems. 

[4:0:66,164] CAL   FIRE cooperates with federal, state and local agencies, local organizations, and private property owners to develop and  

[4:1:66,174] achieve land use objectives. Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using various  

[4:2:66,183] treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods. The Environmental Protection  

[4:3:66,193] Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California Environmental Quality  

[4:4:66,203] Act, the National Environmental Policy Act, and other environmental laws and regulations applicable to departmental actions.  

[4:5:66,213] The Climate and Energy program represents the Department in Natural and Working Lands and Climate Change Scoping Plan  

[4:6:66,223] planning.  

[5:0:66,240] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations:  

[6:0:66,257] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water,  

[6:1:66,267] wildlife, recreation, and other values associated with forest land.   Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting of  

[6:2:66,277] commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and  

[6:3:66,286] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical  

[6:4:66,296] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases, and other environmentally sensitive resources. The program is also  

[6:5:66,306] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of  

[6:6:66,316] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland. Forest Practice program staff also  

[6:7:66,326] assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the effectiveness of the Board's  

[6:8:66,335] forest practice regulations.  

[7:0:66,353] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment:  

[8:0:66,370] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources. Activities  

[8:1:66,380] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support, and meeting certain  

[8:2:66,389] regulatory requirements. In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide fire threat,  

[8:3:66,399] fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators, and urban forestry.  

[9:0:66,416] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program:  

[10:0:66,434] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the Board of Forestry and Fire  

[10:1:66,444] Protection. The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry on non-federal,  

[10:2:66,453] state, and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972. The program also licenses “certified  

[10:3:66,463] specialties” of forestry, with the single certified specialty currently being Certified Rangeland Managers.  

[11:0:66,480] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION  

[12:0:66,498] The Board of Forestry and Fire Protection is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board is responsible for  

[12:1:66,507] developing the general forest policy of the state, determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and representing the state's  

[12:2:66,517] interest in federal forests in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out statutory mandates to protect and  

[12:3:66,527] enhance the state's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory responsibilities are to:  

[13:0:65,544] • Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California;  

[14:0:65,560] • Protect and represent the state's interest in all forestry and rangeland matters;  

[15:0:65,576] • Provide direction and guidance to the Director of CAL FIRE on fire protection and resource management;  

[16:0:65,592] • Deliver a comprehensive regulatory program for forestry and fire protection; and  

[17:0:65,607] • Conduct its duties to inform and respond to the people of California.  

[18:0:66,625] 2480 - DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE LEGAL SERVICES  

[19:0:66,642] The objective of the Department of Justice Legal Services program is to account for the cost of legal services provided by the  

[19:1:66,652] Department of Justice to CAL FIRE.

[12:0:215,574] 3560   State Lands Commission  

[13:0:66,595] The California State Lands Commission provides effective stewardship of the lands, waterways, and resources entrusted to its  

[13:1:66,605] care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and the promotion of public access.  

[13:2:66,614] Execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted in the generation of billions of dollars   in  

[13:3:66,624] revenues, while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy these state lands and resources.  

[13:4:66,642] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands  

[13:5:66,651] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing approximately 4 million acres of California's sovereign  

[13:6:66,661] public trust lands and resources, received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands that the federal  

[13:7:66,671] government conveyed to the state (commonly known as school lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands  

[13:8:66,681] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These trust grants encourage responsible development,  

[13:9:66,690] environmental preservation and enhancement, and public access on the state's tidelands, consistent with the common law  

[13:10:66,700] Public Trust Doctrine. The grants typically require grantees to reinvest revenues generated from the granted lands back into the  

[13:11:66,710] trust to the benefit of all Californians.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 92 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — RES   90 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:180,32] 3560   State Lands Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] Public trust, or sovereign lands, include the beds of natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and  

[3:1:66,78] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays, and the Pacific Ocean extending from the ordinary high  

[3:2:66,88] water mark seaward to the three-mile offshore limit. In addition to sovereign lands, the Commission manages school lands,  

[3:3:66,98] which were granted to California by the federal government in 1853 for the purpose of supporting public education in California.  

[3:4:66,108] School lands typically include the 16th and 36th sections of each township and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the  

[3:5:66,117] five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state, only about 458,843 acres remain in state  

[4:0:66,127] ownership, and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest  

[4:1:66,137] in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.  

[5:0:66,154] Because the State Lands Commission’s (SLC) programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, SLC has a capital outlay  

[5:1:66,164] program to support this need. For the specifics on the SLC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[6:0:66,341] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,360] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[8:0:66,378] The   Mineral Resources Management Division   oversees the efficient and safe development of mineral resources on state lands.  

[8:1:66,387] The state also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oil fields, and as such, the Commission participates in the  

[8:2:66,397] safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas,  

[8:3:66,407] geothermal resources and other solid minerals; collect royalty and other revenues generated therefrom; and ensure that public  

[8:4:66,417] safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these resources  

[8:5:66,426] through engineering review and safety audits. The Commission also implements various programs for the prevention of oil  

[8:6:66,436] spills and the safe operation of offshore facilities and   conducts program and financial audits to ensure that the state receives  

[8:7:66,446] fair value for the development of its resources. The Commission also manages the safe and effective decommissioning of the  

[8:8:66,456] oil and gas facilities under its jurisdiction.  

[9:0:66,473] Further, the Commission manages the mineral resources associated with school lands under its purview, including revenue-

[9:1:66,483] generating extraction and prospecting leases and other projects. The Commission also coordinates with other agencies to  

[9:2:66,493] administer the statewide Abandoned Mines Program.  

[10:0:66,510] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[11:0:66,527] The Land Management Division manages approximately 4 million acres of tide and submerged lands and the beds of natural  

[11:1:66,537] and navigable rivers, streams, lakes, bays, estuaries, inlets, and straits. These lands, often referred to as sovereign or Public  

[11:2:66,547] Trust lands, stretch from the Klamath River and Goose Lake in the north to the Tijuana Estuary in the south, and from   Lake  

[11:3:66,556] Tahoe and   the Colorado River in the east, to three miles offshore from the Pacific Coast   in the west, and include California’s  

[11:4:66,566] two longest rivers, the Sacramento and San Joaquin.  

[12:0:66,584] The Commission also monitors sovereign land granted in trust by the California Legislature to approximately 70 local  

[12:1:66,593] jurisdictions that generally consist of prime waterfront lands and coastal waters, including the state’s five major ports. The  

[12:2:66,603] Commission protects and enhances these lands and natural resources by issuing leases for use or development, preserving  

[12:3:66,613] and expanding public access, and resolving boundaries between public and private lands.  

[13:0:66,630] Finally, the Commission manages the state’s school lands, which seeks to develop and consolidate the state’s school-land  

[13:1:66,640] holdings to the economic benefit of the California State Teachers’ Retirement System.  

[14:0:66,657] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION DIVISION  

[15:0:66,674] The   Marine Environmental Protection Division   regulates marine oil terminals within California, providing the best achievable  

[15:1:66,684] protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Division monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals,  

[15:2:66,694] inspects all marine facilities, and reviews and approves all marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification  

[15:3:66,704] programs. The Division also administers Chapter 31F of the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit  

[15:4:66,714] for their purpose, and implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of nonindigenous  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:1:66,78] research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing  

[3:2:66,88] vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions.  

[3:3:66,105] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[4:0:66,123] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction, and administrative support to the  

[4:1:66,132] Commission’s line programs.

[6:0:197,244] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife  

[7:0:66,265] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and the  

[7:1:66,275] habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological value and for their use and enjoyment by the public.  

[8:0:66,295] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to protect the survival of species and  

[8:1:66,304] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational,  

[8:2:66,314] commercial, scientific, and educational uses.  

[9:0:66,334] Because the Department of Fish and Wildlife programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital  

[9:1:66,343] outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure  

[9:2:66,353] Overview."

[5:0:66,300] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,319] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,339] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the  

[6:1:66,349] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, protection  

[6:2:66,358] and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable populations of  

[6:3:66,368] those species.  

[7:0:66,385] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,403] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses  

[8:1:66,412] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and  

[8:2:66,422] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations  

[8:3:66,432] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations.  

[8:4:66,449] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,467] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological  

[9:1:66,476] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management  

[9:2:66,486] of fish and wildlife.  

[9:3:66,503] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT  

[10:0:66,523] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety, and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes  

[10:1:66,533] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; and investigates habitat destruction, pollution  

[10:2:66,542] incidents and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid,  

[10:3:66,552] and homeland security.  

[11:0:66,569] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM  

[12:0:66,589] This program provides resource conservation education and activities in the classroom and on public and private lands;  

[12:1:66,599] community and stakeholder outreach; and information and data using a variety of methods including publications,  

[12:2:66,609] presentations, web applications, and media relations.  

[12:3:66,626] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,645] This program minimizes damage and environmental impacts to, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife  

[13:1:66,655] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland  

[13:2:66,665] habitats.  

[13:3:66,682] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION  

[14:0:66,699] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by  

[14:1:66,709] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:198,588] 3720   California Coastal Commission  

[12:0:66,609] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed by the Governor, the Senate Rules  

[12:1:66,619] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the  

[12:2:66,629] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and  

[12:3:66,638] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources;  

[12:4:66,648] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. The Commission is also designated the  

[12:5:66,658] principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone Management Act in  

[12:6:66,668] California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal development projects,  

[12:7:66,677] and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be subject to state control.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:163,32] 3720   California Coastal Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,87] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,107] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and  

[4:1:66,117] regulation. Activities include:  

[5:0:65,134] • Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and  

[5:1:76,144] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[6:0:65,160] • Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of  

[6:1:76,169] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local  

[6:2:76,179] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission.  

[6:3:65,195] • Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits.  

[6:4:65,211] • Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[6:5:65,226] • Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation.  

[6:6:65,242] • Implementing a coastal water quality protection program.  

[6:7:65,258] • Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management.  

[6:8:65,274] • Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program.  

[7:0:65,290] • Addressing coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas development, offshore wind projects,  

[7:1:76,299] electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural gas facilities.  

[7:2:65,315] • Providing administrative support including accounting, budgeting, business services, support services, information  

[7:3:76,325] technology, and human resources services.

[7:0:66,506] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,525] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[7:2:66,542] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals:  

[7:3:65,559] • Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California coast  

[7:4:76,569] and around San Francisco Bay.  

[8:0:65,585] • Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational  

[8:1:76,595] amenities.  

[9:0:65,610] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[9:1:65,626] • Support education projects and programs related to the preservation, enhancement, and enjoyment of coastal resources.  

[9:2:65,642] • Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles of  

[9:3:76,652] the coast.  

[9:4:66,669] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,688] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project  

[10:1:66,698] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State  

[10:2:66,708] Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program and supports programs  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] that increase public access to the coast. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of

[3:2:66,88] 33,000 acres of habitat.

[4:1:66,117] miles of new trails.  

[5:0:66,135] State Coastal Conservancy funds are primarily expended for purposes of local assistance but may also be expended for capital  

[5:1:66,144] outlay purposes, see Program 2820.  

[5:2:66,162] 2820 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY CAPITAL OUTLAY PROGRAM  

[5:3:66,179] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to state agencies for the acquisition of land and  

[5:4:66,189] easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State Coastal Conservancy helps to  

[5:5:66,198] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of  

[5:6:66,208] more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements and the Conservancy has  

[5:7:66,218] helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat.

[13:0:183,599] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation  

[14:0:66,620] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and  

[14:1:66,630] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most  

[14:2:66,640] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and  

[14:3:66,650] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to the  

[14:4:66,660] major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural  

[14:5:66,669] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park  

[14:6:66,679] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and  

[14:7:66,689] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding,  

[14:8:66,699] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds,  

[14:9:66,708] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by providing  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:177,99] 3810   Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy  

[2:0:66,120] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim of  

[2:1:66,130] the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, and  

[2:2:66,139] conservation purposes. The Conservancy may:  

[2:3:65,157] • Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes.  

[3:0:65,172] • Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or  

[3:1:76,182] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with and  

[3:2:76,192] does not adversely impact the environment.  

[4:0:65,208] • Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing  

[4:1:76,217] recreational opportunities for all residents.  

[4:2:65,233] • Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail.  

[5:0:65,249] • Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River  

[5:1:76,259] Watershed.
