[2:0:69,32] 3820   San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional  

[3:1:66,78] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance the   San Francisco Bay, and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive  

[3:2:66,88] use. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan, and relies on the McAteer-Petris Act and other  

[3:3:66,98] statutory and regulatory authorities to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The Commission issues permits  

[3:4:66,108] for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and within salt ponds and certain  

[3:5:66,117] managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh Preservation Act of 1977. By  

[3:6:66,127] statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management Act's program for the Bay and  

[3:7:66,137] exercises authority over federal activities otherwise not subject to state control. The Commission leads the ongoing multi-

[3:8:66,147] agency regional effort to address the impacts of rising sea level and climate change on the Bay and along the shoreline.  

[4:0:66,176] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[5:0:81,153] 3825   San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy  

[5:1:66,174] The San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy was established in 1999 to acquire and manage  

[5:2:66,184] public lands. Through these acquisitions, the Conservancy provides for additional open space, low-impact recreational uses,  

[5:3:66,193] educational uses, water conservation and watershed improvement, and wildlife and habitat restoration and protection. These  

[5:4:66,203] acquisitions also help to preserve the San Gabriel Mountains and the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and their  

[5:5:66,213] tributaries, consistent with existing and adopted river and flood control projects for the protection of life and property.

[3:0:66,68] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance, and enrich the overall quality of the Delta  

[3:1:66,78] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation, and natural resources,   while remaining   mindful of the  

[3:2:66,88] importance of the Delta to all Californians. The Commission   supports orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta  

[3:3:66,98] land resources and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the  

[3:4:66,108] unique cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes recommendations  

[3:5:66,117] on methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place and promotes Delta legacy communities, regional economic  

[3:6:66,127] sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.

[6:0:202,222] 3845   San Diego River Conservancy  

[7:0:66,243] The mission of the San Diego River Conservancy is to implement the San Diego River Conservancy Act through programs  

[7:1:66,253] emphasizing Land Conservation, Recreation and Education, Natural and Cultural Resources, Water Quality and Natural Flood  

[7:2:66,262] Conveyance, Organizational Capacity, and the San Diego Rivers Watershed Consortium. This Conservancy’s mission will be  

[7:3:66,272] accomplished in part by building with our partners the San Diego River Trail stretching over 52 miles from the headwaters in  

[7:4:66,282] Julian to the Pacific Ocean. The Conservancy has established advisory panels to assist in restoring and enhancing three  

[7:5:66,292] nearby watersheds: the Otay River, Sweetwater River, and Tijuana River. Working with stakeholders within these watersheds  

[7:6:66,302] fosters collaboration among governments, public agencies and nonprofit organizations, and leverages grant funding to achieve  

[7:7:66,311] an array of conservation objectives for the region with an emphasis on native habitat restoration and mitigating the impacts of  

[7:8:66,321] climate change, seawater intrusion in local watersheds, sea level rise, and the risk of wildfire and flooding.

[11:0:169,537] 3850   Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy  

[12:0:66,558] The mission of the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy is to acquire and hold as open space the mountainous lands  

[12:1:66,568] surrounding the Coachella Valley and the natural lands within the Coachella Valley. In addition, the Conservancy works to  

[12:2:66,578] protect wildlife and enhance recreational and educational experiences on those lands, to conserve local water resources and to  

[12:3:66,588] promote sustainability and climate change adaptability within the desert and surrounding mountain areas.

[5:0:66,226] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,245] 3220 - SIERRA NEVADA CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,264] Established pursuant to Chapter 726, Statutes of 2004, the Conservancy is authorized to:  

[6:1:65,282] • Provide increased opportunities for tourism and recreation.  

[7:0:65,297] • Protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, cultural, archaeological, historical, and living resources.  

[7:1:65,313] • Aid in the preservation of working landscapes.  

[7:2:65,329] • Reduce the risk of natural disasters, such as wildfires.  

[7:3:65,345] • Protect and improve water and air quality.  

[8:0:65,360] • Assist the regional economy through the operation of the Conservancy's program.  

[8:1:65,376] • Undertake efforts to enhance public use and enjoyment of lands owned by the public.  

[9:0:66,394] In support of these programs, the Conservancy facilitates collaborative planning efforts with local and regional governments,  

[9:1:66,403] community groups and interested parties, provides technical assistance, and initiates and funds projects to identify and fill  

[9:2:66,413] critical needs in the Sierra Nevada region.

[2:0:173,32] 3855   Sierra Nevada Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:196,99] 3860   Department of Water Resources  

[4:0:66,120] The Department of Water Resources protects, conserves, develops, and manages California's water supply. The Department  

[4:1:66,130] evaluates existing water resources, forecasts future water needs, and explores potential solutions to meet the state’s growing  

[4:2:66,139] needs for personal use, irrigation, industry, recreation, power generation, and fish and wildlife. The Department also works to  

[5:0:66,149] prevent and minimize flood damage, oversee the safety of dams, and educate the public about the importance of water and its  

[5:1:66,159] efficient use.  

[6:0:66,178] Because the Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to  

[6:1:66,188] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."  

[7:0:66,218] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[6:0:66,256] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,275] 3230 - CONTINUING FORMULATION OF THE CALIFORNIA WATER PLAN  

[8:0:66,292] The California Water Plan is the state's strategic plan for the efficient use, management and development of the state's water  

[8:1:66,302] resources. The Plan is updated every five years and provides a framework for water managers, legislators, and the public to  

[8:2:66,312] consider options and make decisions regarding California's water future. The Plan evaluates current and future water  

[8:3:66,322] conditions, challenges and opportunities. It presents basic data and information on California's water resources including water  

[8:4:66,332] supply evaluations and assessments of existing and future agricultural, urban, and environmental water uses. The Plan  

[8:5:66,341] identifies and evaluates multiple future scenarios and over 30 resource management strategies such as conservation,  

[8:6:66,351] recycling, desalination, transfers, storage, conveyance, water quality, watershed management, ecosystem restoration,  

[8:7:66,361] groundwater, and urban land use management to help meet future demands in light of uncertainties and unexpected  

[8:8:66,371] catastrophic events. This program also identifies ways for the state to: (1) help local agencies and governments prepare  

[8:9:66,380] integrated regional water management plans on a watershed basis and diversify their regional water portfolios to ensure  

[8:10:66,390] sustainable water uses, reliable water supplies, better water quality, environmental stewardship, efficient urban development,  

[8:11:66,400] protection of agriculture, and supporting a strong economy, (2) help cities, counties and local agencies prepare a Water  

[8:12:66,410] Element for their General Plans, Urban Water Management Plans and Agricultural Water Management Plans, and (3) help local  

[8:13:66,420] agencies and tribal governments improve water and land use planning coordination.  

[9:0:66,437] 3240 - IMPLEMENTATION OF THE STATE WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT SYSTEM  

[10:0:66,454] The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage facilities (reservoirs and lakes), 21  

[10:1:66,464] pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700 miles of conveyance (canals,  

[10:2:66,474] pipelines, and tunnels). The Project provides water to over 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated farmland.  

[11:0:66,491] The Department plans, designs, constructs, operates, maintains, and manages State Water Project facilities that provide water  

[11:1:66,501] to facilities located from Plumas County to Riverside County.  

[12:0:66,518] The Delta Conveyance Program is charged with improving the Delta ecosystem and ensuring water supply reliability in a safe,  

[12:1:66,528] timely, and cost-effective manner. This includes implementation of the Delta Conveyance, project specific environmental  

[12:2:66,538] commitments, and compliance with the Environmental Impact Report and Environmental Impact Statement and other state and  

[12:3:66,547] federal regulations and permits.  

[13:0:66,565] 3245 - PUBLIC SAFETY AND PREVENTION OF DAMAGE  

[14:0:66,582] This program supports the California Water Action Plan by protecting life and property from damage by floods, ensures proper  

[14:1:66,592] construction and maintenance of jurisdictional dams and levees, and provides loans for levee construction. Activities include  

[14:2:66,601] assessing the state and regional investment needs to reduce risk, preventive floodplain management to discourage unwise use  

[14:3:66,611] of areas subject to flooding, protection of floodplains, issuance of flood warnings, operation of flood control facilities,  

[14:4:66,621] coordination and supervision of flood fight activities, and annual levee and flood channel maintenance and inspection in  

[14:5:66,631] cooperation with other local, state, and federal partner agencies. This program also buys land, easements, and rights-of-way  

[14:6:66,640] for federal flood control projects and supervises the design and construction of new dams and periodic inspection and the  

[14:7:66,650] reevaluation of all existing jurisdictional dams for proper operation and maintenance. Fiscal oversight and coordination activities  

[14:8:66,660] associated with the Disaster Preparedness and Flood Prevention Bond Act of 2006 are administered under this program. The  

[14:9:66,670] program also reviews federal dam projects in coordination with federal and other state agencies with regard to dam safety.  

[15:0:66,687] 3250 - CENTRAL VALLEY FLOOD PROTECTION BOARD  

[16:0:66,707] The Central Valley Flood Protection Board has regulatory authority over the State Plan of Flood Control (SPFC) facilities,  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] designated floodways, and regulated streams in the Central Valley. The Board regulates encroachments on the system by  

[3:1:66,78] issuing permits and initiating enforcement action when necessary to maintain the integrity of the levees and floodways that  

[3:2:66,88] protect the valley's people and property. The Board manages the state's portfolio of real property held by the Sacramento-San  

[3:3:66,98] Joaquin Drainage District. The Board serves as the non-federal sponsor to the United States Army Corps of Engineers on large  

[3:4:66,108] joint state-federal levee improvement projects and assists the more than   100 local maintaining agencies that operate and  

[3:5:66,117] maintain the SPFC. The Board conducts regular public meetings, workshops and tours, providing a public forum for  

[3:6:66,127] stakeholders.  

[3:7:66,144] 3255 - SERVICES  

[4:0:66,162] This program provides technical support within the Department and expertise in the fields of water resources planning,  

[4:1:66,171] development and management; watermaster services; scientific analyses performed by DWR's chemical laboratory;  

[4:2:66,181] information technology; and mapping, surveying and engineering services for other agencies.  

[4:3:66,198] 3260 - CALIFORNIA ENERGY BOND OFFICE  

[5:0:66,216] California Energy Bond Office is responsible for managing the Electrical Power Fund and the Department of Water Resources  

[5:1:66,226] (DWR) Charge Fund. The Electric Power Fund was created in response to the 2001 California energy crisis; for a limited period  

[5:2:66,235] of time, the Electrical Power Fund purchased electric power on behalf of the state's investor-owned utilities and issued $11.3  

[5:3:66,245] billion in bonds. The final amount of the outstanding bonds were defeased in September 2020. Bond proceeds were used to  

[5:4:66,255] repay amounts borrowed for power purchases during the 2001 energy crisis.   The DWR Charge Fund was created in 2019 by  

[5:5:66,265] Chapter 79, Statutes of 2019 (AB 1054) to collect remittances from charges on investor-owned utilities ratepayers pursuant to  

[5:6:66,274] Water Code Section 80550.

[6:0:66,226] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,245] The Department of Water Resources has two major infrastructure programs: the State Water Project and the flood control  

[7:1:66,255] system in the Central Valley. The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage  

[7:2:66,264] facilities, reservoirs, and lakes, 21 pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700  

[7:3:66,274] miles of canals, pipelines, and tunnels. The Project provides water to 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated  

[7:4:66,284] farmland. The Project also provides recreational opportunities to approximately 6 million visitors annually at 15 separate  

[7:5:66,294] reservoir-based facilities as well as public fishing access to much of the Aqueduct. Existing flood control infrastructure in the  

[7:6:66,303] Central Valley consists of 1,595 miles of levees, 348,000 acres of channels and floodways, over 800,000 linear feet of bank  

[7:7:66,313] protection, over 60 mitigation and environmental restoration sites, and 55 various flood control structures including dams, weirs,  

[7:8:66,323] pumping plants, diversion structures, gate structures, and drop structures. In general, these facilities provide more flood  

[7:9:66,333] protection in urbanized areas and less in agricultural areas.

[10:0:159,598] 3875   Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy  

[11:0:66,619] The mission of the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy is to act as the primary state agency to implement ecosystem  

[11:1:66,629] restoration in the Delta and   support efforts that advance both environmental protection and the economic well-being of Delta  

[11:2:66,639] residents in a complementary manner. The Conservancy's activities include protecting and enhancing habitat and habitat  

[11:3:66,649] restoration; protecting and preserving Delta agriculture and working landscapes; providing increased opportunities for tourism  

[11:4:66,658] and recreation; promoting Delta legacy communities and economic vitality in the Delta; increasing the resilience of the Delta to  

[11:5:66,668] the effects of climate change and natural disasters such as floods and earthquakes; protecting and improving water quality;  

[11:6:66,678] assisting the Delta regional economy;   conducting activities to protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, agricultural,  

[11:7:66,688] cultural, historical, and living resources; assisting local entities in the implementation of their habitat conservation plans and  

[11:8:66,698] natural community conservation plans; facilitating protection and safe harbor agreements under the federal Endangered

[11:0:211,555] 3885   Delta Stewardship Council  

[12:0:66,576] Established in 2009 by the Delta Reform Act, the mission of the Delta Stewardship Council, through a seven-member board, is  

[12:1:66,585] to further the state's coequal goals of providing a more reliable water supply for California and protecting, restoring, and  

[12:2:66,595] enhancing the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta ecosystem. The goals are to be achieved in a manner that protects and  

[12:3:66,605] enhances the unique cultural, recreational, natural resource, and agricultural values of the Delta. In 2013, the Council adopted  

[12:4:66,615] a legally enforceable Delta Plan to further the coequal goals and guide state and local agency activities related to the Delta.  

[12:5:66,625] Under state law, agencies are required to coordinate their actions pursuant to the Delta Plan with the Council and the other  

[12:6:66,634] relevant agencies.   The Council is informed by scientific input from the Delta Science Program and the Delta Independent  

[12:7:66,644] Science Board. The mission of the Delta Science Program is to provide the best possible unbiased scientific information to  

[12:8:66,654] inform water and environmental decisionmaking in the Delta.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.
